<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:United_States_space_program_sidebar" title="Template:United States space program sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:United_States_space_program_sidebar" title="Template talk:United States space program sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:United_States_space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>National Aeronautics and Space Administration</b> (<b>NASA</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is an <a href="/wiki/Independent_agency_of_the_United_States_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent agency of the United States government">independent agency</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">executive branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">United States federal government</a> responsible for the civilian <a href="/wiki/List_of_space_agencies" class="mw-redirect" title="List of space agencies">space program</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Aeronautics" title="Aeronautics">aeronautics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> research.<sup id="cite_ref-independent_agency_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent_agency-10">[note 1]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> established NASA in 1958<sup id="cite_ref-DDE_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDE-11">[10]</a></sup> with a distinctly civilian (rather than military) orientation encouraging peaceful applications in <a href="/wiki/Space_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Space science">space science</a>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Act" title="National Aeronautics and Space Act">National Aeronautics and Space Act</a> was passed on July 29, 1958, disestablishing NASA's predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/National_Advisory_Committee_for_Aeronautics" title="National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics">National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics</a> (NACA). The new agency became operational on October 1, 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-act1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-act1-12">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NacaNASA_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NacaNASA-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since that time, most US <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a> efforts have been led by NASA, including the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo</a> <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">Moon landing</a> missions, the <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> space station, and later the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>. Currently, NASA is supporting the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> and is overseeing the development of the <a href="/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft)" title="Orion (spacecraft)">Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew</a> vehicles. The agency is also responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Launch_Services_Program" title="Launch Services Program">Launch Services Program</a> (LSP) which provides oversight of launch operations and countdown management for unmanned NASA launches.</p>
<p>NASA science is focused on better understanding Earth through the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Observing_System" title="Earth Observing System">Earth Observing System</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> advancing <a href="/wiki/Heliophysics" title="Heliophysics">heliophysics</a> through the efforts of the Science Mission Directorate's Heliophysics Research Program,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> exploring bodies throughout the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> with advanced <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic spacecraft</a> missions such as <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20150828_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150828-16">[15]</a></sup> and researching <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a> topics, such as the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Great_Observatories_program" title="Great Observatories program">Great Observatories</a> and associated programs.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> NASA shares data with various national and international organizations such as from the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Gases_Observing_Satellite" title="Greenhouse Gases Observing Satellite">Greenhouse Gases Observing Satellite</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation">Creation</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Creation_of_NASA" title="Creation of NASA">Creation of NASA</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg/310px-Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg" width="310" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg/465px-Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg/620px-Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2832" data-file-height="2206" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Receives_Mariner_2_Model.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bill_Pickering_(rocket_scientist)" title="Bill Pickering (rocket scientist)">William H. Pickering</a>, (center) JPL Director, President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, (right). NASA Administrator <a href="/wiki/James_E._Webb" title="James E. Webb">James E. Webb</a> (background) discussing the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner program</a>, with a model presented.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1946, the <a href="/wiki/National_Advisory_Committee_for_Aeronautics" title="National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics">National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics</a> (NACA) had been experimenting with rocket planes such as the supersonic <a href="/wiki/Bell_X-1" title="Bell X-1">Bell X-1</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NACASupersonic_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NACASupersonic-18">[17]</a></sup> In the early 1950s, there was challenge to launch an artificial satellite for the <a href="/wiki/International_Geophysical_Year" title="International Geophysical Year">International Geophysical Year</a> (1957–58). An effort for this was the American <a href="/wiki/Project_Vanguard" title="Project Vanguard">Project Vanguard</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet</a> launch of the world's first artificial <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a></i>) on October 4, 1957, the attention of the United States turned toward its own fledgling space efforts. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">US Congress</a>, alarmed by the perceived threat to national security and technological leadership (known as the "<a href="/wiki/Sputnik_crisis" title="Sputnik crisis">Sputnik crisis</a>"), urged immediate and swift action; President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> and his advisers counseled more deliberate measures. This led to an agreement that a new federal agency mainly based on NACA was needed to conduct all non-military activity in space. The <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">Advanced Research Projects Agency</a> (ARPA) was created in February 1958 to develop space technology for military application.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 29, 1958, Eisenhower signed the <a href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Act" title="National Aeronautics and Space Act">National Aeronautics and Space Act</a>, establishing NASA. When it began operations on October 1, 1958, NASA absorbed the 43-year-old NACA intact; its 8,000 employees, an annual budget of US$100&#160;million, three major research laboratories (<a href="/wiki/Langley_Aeronautical_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Langley Aeronautical Laboratory">Langley Aeronautical Laboratory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ames_Aeronautical_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Ames Aeronautical Laboratory">Ames Aeronautical Laboratory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Flight_Propulsion_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis Flight Propulsion Laboratory">Lewis Flight Propulsion Laboratory</a>) and two small test facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Glennan_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glennan-20">[19]</a></sup> A <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Logos_of_NASA" class="extiw" title="commons:Logos of NASA">NASA seal</a> was approved by President Eisenhower in 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-order_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-order-21">[20]</a></sup> Elements of the <a href="/wiki/Army_Ballistic_Missile_Agency" title="Army Ballistic Missile Agency">Army Ballistic Missile Agency</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Research_Laboratory" title="United States Naval Research Laboratory">United States Naval Research Laboratory</a> were incorporated into NASA. A significant contributor to NASA's entry into the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> with the Soviet Union was the technology from the <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">German rocket program</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a>, who was now working for the <a href="/wiki/Army_Ballistic_Missile_Agency" title="Army Ballistic Missile Agency">Army Ballistic Missile Agency</a> (ABMA), which in turn incorporated the technology of American scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Goddard" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Goddard">Robert Goddard</a>'s earlier works.<sup id="cite_ref-recoll_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-recoll-22">[21]</a></sup> Earlier research efforts within the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">US Air Force</a><sup id="cite_ref-Glennan_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glennan-20">[19]</a></sup> and many of ARPA's early space programs were also transferred to NASA.<sup id="cite_ref-DARPA_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DARPA-23">[22]</a></sup> In December 1958, NASA gained control of the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>, a contractor facility operated by the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Glennan_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glennan-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_flight_programs">Space flight programs</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg/220px-VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg/330px-VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg/440px-VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="562" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VonBraunMuellerReesSA6.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
At launch control for the May 28, 1964, <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I#Saturn_I_launches" title="Saturn I">Saturn I SA-6 launch</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> is at center.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_NASA_missions" title="List of NASA missions">List of NASA missions</a></div>
<p>NASA has conducted many manned and unmanned spaceflight programs throughout its history. Unmanned programs launched the first American artificial <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> into Earth orbit for scientific and <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications</a> purposes, and sent scientific probes to explore the planets of the solar system, starting with <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, and including "<a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">grand tours</a>" of the outer planets. Manned programs sent the first Americans into <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO), won the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> by landing twelve men on the Moon from 1969 to 1972 in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, developed a semi-reusable LEO <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>, and developed LEO <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> capability by itself and with the cooperation of several other nations including post-Soviet <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. Some missions include both manned and unmanned aspects, such as the Galileo probe, which was deployed by astronauts in Earth orbit before being sent unmanned to Jupiter.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_programs">Manned programs</span></h3>
<p>The experimental <a href="/wiki/Rocket-powered_aircraft" title="Rocket-powered aircraft">rocket-powered aircraft</a> programs started by NACA were extended by NASA as support for manned spaceflight. This was followed by a one-man <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> program, and in turn by a two-man capsule program. Reacting to loss of national prestige and <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">security</a> fears caused by early leads in space exploration by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, in 1961 President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> proposed the ambitious goal "of landing a man on the Moon by the end of [the 1960s], and returning him safely to the Earth." This goal was met in 1969 by the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, and NASA planned even more ambitious activities leading to a <a href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" class="mw-redirect" title="Manned mission to Mars">manned mission to Mars</a>. However, reduction of the perceived threat and changing political priorities almost immediately caused the termination of most of these plans. NASA turned its attention to an Apollo-derived temporary space laboratory, and a semi-reusable Earth orbital shuttle. In the 1990s, funding was approved for NASA to develop a permanent Earth orbital <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> in cooperation with the international community, which now included the former rival, post-Soviet <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. To date, NASA has launched a total of 166 manned space missions on rockets, and thirteen <a href="/wiki/X-15" class="mw-redirect" title="X-15">X-15</a> rocket flights above the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">USAF</a> definition of spaceflight altitude, 260,000 feet (80&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="X-15_rocket_plane_.281959.E2.80.9368.29">X-15 rocket plane (1959–68)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">North American X-15</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:X-15_in_flight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/X-15_in_flight.jpg/220px-X-15_in_flight.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/X-15_in_flight.jpg/330px-X-15_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/X-15_in_flight.jpg/440px-X-15_in_flight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1992" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:X-15_in_flight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
X-15 in powered flight</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">X-15</a> was an NACA experimental rocket-powered <a href="/wiki/Hypersonic_speed" title="Hypersonic speed">hypersonic</a> research aircraft, developed in conjunction with the US Air Force and Navy. The design featured a slender fuselage with fairings along the side containing fuel and early computerized control systems.<sup id="cite_ref-AerospacewebX15_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AerospacewebX15-25">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/RFP" class="mw-redirect" title="RFP">Requests for proposal</a> were issued on December 30, 1954, for the airframe, and February 4, 1955, for the rocket engine. The airframe contract was awarded to <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> in November 1955, and the XLR30 engine contract was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Reaction_Motors" title="Reaction Motors">Reaction Motors</a> in 1956, and three planes were built. The X-15 was <a href="/wiki/Drop_test" title="Drop test">drop-launched</a> from the wing of one of two NASA <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">Boeing B-52 Stratofortresses</a>, <i>NB52A</i> tail number 52-003, and <i>NB52B</i>, tail number 52-008 (known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Balls_8" title="Balls 8">Balls 8</a></i>). Release took place at an altitude of about 45,000 feet (14&#160;km) and a speed of about 500 miles per hour (805&#160;km/h).</p>
<p>Twelve pilots were selected for the program from the Air Force, Navy, and NACA (later NASA). A total of 199 flights were made between 1959 and 1968, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Flight_airspeed_record" title="Flight airspeed record">official world record</a> for the highest speed ever reached by a manned powered aircraft (current as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=NASA&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>), and a maximum speed of Mach 6.72, 4,519 miles per hour (7,273&#160;km/h).<sup id="cite_ref-Fastest_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fastest-26">[25]</a></sup> The altitude record for X-15 was 354,200 feet (107.96&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-NASAHyper_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHyper-27">[26]</a></sup> Eight of the pilots were awarded Air Force <a href="/wiki/Astronaut_Badge" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronaut Badge">astronaut wings</a> for flying above 260,000 feet (80&#160;km), and two flights by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_A._Walker" title="Joseph A. Walker">Joseph A. Walker</a> exceeded 100 kilometers (330,000&#160;ft), qualifying as spaceflight according to the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="Fédération Aéronautique Internationale">International Aeronautical Federation</a>. The X-15 program employed mechanical techniques used in the later manned spaceflight programs, including <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> jets for controlling the orientation of a spacecraft, <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suits</a>, and horizon definition for navigation.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAHyper_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHyper-27">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">reentry</a> and landing data collected were valuable to NASA for designing the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AerospacewebX15_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AerospacewebX15-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Mercury_.281959.E2.80.9363.29">Project Mercury (1959–63)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glenn62.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/220px-Glenn62.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/330px-Glenn62.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/440px-Glenn62.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1528" data-file-height="1127" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glenn62.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Glenn on Friendship 7: first US orbital flight, 1962</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> began, an early objective was to get a person into Earth orbit as soon as possible, therefore the simplest spacecraft that could be launched by existing rockets was favored. The US Air Force's <a href="/wiki/Man_in_Space_Soonest" title="Man in Space Soonest">Man in Space Soonest</a> program considered many manned spacecraft designs, ranging from rocket planes like the X-15, to small ballistic <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsules</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Project1969_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Project1969-28">[27]</a></sup> By 1958, the space plane concepts were eliminated in favor of the ballistic capsule.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>When NASA was created that same year, the Air Force program was transferred to it and renamed <i>Project Mercury</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">first seven astronauts</a> were selected among candidates from the Navy, Air Force and Marine test pilot programs. On May 5, 1961, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> became the first American in space aboard <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_3" title="Mercury-Redstone 3">Freedom&#160;7</a></i>, launched by a <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_Launch_Vehicle" title="Mercury-Redstone Launch Vehicle">Redstone booster</a> on a 15-minute <a href="/wiki/Ballistics" title="Ballistics">ballistic</a> (suborbital) flight.<sup id="cite_ref-ShepardsRide_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShepardsRide-30">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> became the first American to be launched into <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> by an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_LV-3B" title="Atlas LV-3B">Atlas launch vehicle</a> on February 20, 1962, aboard <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_6" title="Mercury-Atlas 6"><i>Friendship&#160;7</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AnAmericaninOrbit_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AnAmericaninOrbit-31">[30]</a></sup> Glenn completed three orbits, after which three more orbital flights were made, culminating in <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Cooper" title="Gordon Cooper">L. Gordon Cooper</a>'s 22-orbit flight <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_9" title="Mercury-Atlas 9">Faith 7</a></i>, May 15–16, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAManned_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAManned-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> (USSR) competed with its own single-pilot spacecraft, <a href="/wiki/Vostok_programme" title="Vostok programme">Vostok</a>. They sent the first man in space, by launching cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> into a single Earth orbit aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">Vostok 1</a> in April 1961, one month before Shepard's flight.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAGagarin_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAGagarin-33">[32]</a></sup> In August 1962, they achieved an almost four-day record flight with <a href="/wiki/Andriyan_Nikolayev" title="Andriyan Nikolayev">Andriyan Nikolayev</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_3" title="Vostok 3">Vostok 3</a>, and also conducted a concurrent <a href="/wiki/Vostok_4" title="Vostok 4">Vostok 4</a> mission carrying <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Popovich" title="Pavel Popovich">Pavel Popovich</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Gemini_.281961.E2.80.9366.29">Project Gemini (1961–66)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg/220px-Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg/330px-Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg/440px-Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3802" data-file-height="3085" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ed White on Gemini 4: first US <a href="/wiki/Spacewalk" class="mw-redirect" title="Spacewalk">spacewalk</a>, 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Based on studies to grow the Mercury spacecraft capabilities to long-duration flights, developing <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> techniques, and precision Earth landing, Project Gemini was started as a two-man program in 1962 to overcome the Soviets' lead and to support the Apollo manned lunar landing program, adding <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA) and <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">rendezvous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">docking</a> to its objectives. The first manned Gemini flight, <a href="/wiki/Gemini_3" title="Gemini 3">Gemini 3</a>, was flown by <a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">Gus Grissom</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">John Young</a> on March 23, 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-TheLastHurdle_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheLastHurdle-34">[33]</a></sup> Nine missions followed in 1965 and 1966, demonstrating an endurance mission of nearly fourteen days, rendezvous, docking, and practical EVA, and gathering medical data on the effects of weightlessness on humans.<sup id="cite_ref-PlansforGemini3_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlansforGemini3-35">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AnAlternativeTarget_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AnAlternativeTarget-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Premier of the Soviet Union">Soviet Premier</a> <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>, the USSR competed with Gemini by converting their Vostok spacecraft into a two- or three-man <a href="/wiki/Voskhod_(spacecraft)" title="Voskhod (spacecraft)">Voskhod</a>. They succeeded in launching two manned flights before Gemini's first flight, achieving a three-cosmonaut flight in 1963 and the first EVA in 1964. After this, the program was canceled, and Gemini caught up while spacecraft designer <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">Sergei Korolev</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz spacecraft</a>, their answer to Apollo.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo_program_.281961.E2.80.9372.29">Apollo program (1961–72)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg/220px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg/330px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg/440px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2349" data-file-height="1714" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apollo 11: Buzz Aldrin on the Moon, 1969.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The U.S public's perception of the Soviet lead in the space race (by putting the first man in space) motivated President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> to ask the Congress on May 25, 1961, to commit the federal government to a program to land a man on the Moon by the end of the 1960s, which effectively launched the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apollo was one of the most expensive American scientific programs ever. It cost more than $20 billion in 1960s dollars<sup id="cite_ref-Butts_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butts-38">[37]</a></sup> or an estimated $208&#160;billion in present-day US dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-39">[38]</a></sup> (In comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> cost roughly $26.6&#160;billion, accounting for inflation.)<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-39">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harv_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harv-40">[39]</a></sup> It used the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_rocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Saturn rocket">Saturn rockets</a> as launch vehicles, which were far bigger than the rockets built for previous projects.<sup id="cite_ref-AstroSat5_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AstroSat5-41">[40]</a></sup> The spacecraft was also bigger; it had two main parts, the combined command and service module (CSM) and the lunar landing module (LM). The LM was to be left on the Moon and only the command module (CM) containing the three astronauts would eventually return to Earth.</p>
<p>The second manned mission, <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a>, brought astronauts for the first time in a flight around the Moon in December 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAApol8_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAApol8-42">[41]</a></sup> Shortly before, the Soviets had sent an unmanned spacecraft around the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-Siddiqi_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siddiqi-43">[42]</a></sup> On the next two missions docking maneuvers that were needed for the Moon landing were practiced<sup id="cite_ref-NasaApollo9_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NasaApollo9-44">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASAApollo10_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAApollo10-45">[44]</a></sup> and then finally the Moon landing was made on the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> mission in July 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-NasaApollo11_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NasaApollo11-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg/220px-NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg/330px-NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg/440px-NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1088" data-file-height="755" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apollo 17: <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">LRV</a>-003, 1972.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">person to stand on the Moon</a> was <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, who was followed by <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a> orbited above. Five subsequent Apollo missions also landed astronauts on the Moon, the last in December 1972. Throughout these six Apollo spaceflights, twelve men walked on the Moon. These missions returned a wealth of scientific data and 381.7 kilograms (842&#160;lb) of lunar samples. Topics covered by experiments performed included <a href="/wiki/Soil_mechanics" title="Soil mechanics">soil mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">heat flow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment" class="mw-redirect" title="Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment">lunar ranging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> The Moon landing marked the end of the space race; and as a gesture, Armstrong mentioned mankind when he stepped down on the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apollo set major <a href="/wiki/List_of_space_exploration_milestones,_1957-1969" class="mw-redirect" title="List of space exploration milestones, 1957-1969">milestones</a> in human spaceflight. It stands alone in sending manned missions beyond <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>, and landing humans on another <a href="/wiki/Celestial_body" class="mw-redirect" title="Celestial body">celestial body</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> was the first manned spacecraft to orbit another celestial body, while <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a> marked the last moonwalk and the last manned mission beyond <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> to date. The program spurred advances in many areas of technology peripheral to rocketry and manned spaceflight, including <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a>, telecommunications, and computers. Apollo sparked interest in many fields of engineering and left many physical facilities and machines developed for the program as landmarks. Many objects and artifacts from the program are on display at various locations throughout the world, notably at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">Smithsonian's Air and Space Museums</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab_.281965.E2.80.9379.29">Skylab (1965–79)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg/220px-Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg/330px-Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg/440px-Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2875" data-file-height="1998" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skylab space station, 1974</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Skylab was the United States' first and only independently built <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-skylabFirst_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skylabFirst-50">[49]</a></sup> Conceived in 1965 as a workshop to be constructed in space from a spent <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a> upper stage, the 169,950&#160;lb (77,088&#160;kg) station was constructed on Earth and launched on May 14, 1973, atop the first two stages of a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>, into a 235-nautical-mile (435&#160;km) orbit inclined at 50° to the equator. Damaged during launch by the loss of its thermal protection and one electricity-generating solar panel, it was repaired to functionality by its first crew. It was occupied for a total of 171 days by 3 successive crews in 1973 and 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-skylabFirst_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skylabFirst-50">[49]</a></sup> It included a laboratory for studying the effects of <a href="/wiki/Microgravity_environment" class="mw-redirect" title="Microgravity environment">microgravity</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">solar observatory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-skylabFirst_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skylabFirst-50">[49]</a></sup> NASA planned to have a Space Shuttle dock with it, and elevate Skylab to a higher safe altitude, but the Shuttle was not ready for flight before Skylab's re-entry on July 11, 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-livingandworking_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livingandworking-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>To save cost, NASA used one of the Saturn V rockets originally earmarked for a canceled Apollo mission to launch the Skylab. Apollo spacecraft were used for transporting astronauts to and from the station. Three three-man crews stayed aboard the station for periods of 28, 59, and 84 days. Skylab's habitable volume was 11,290 cubic feet (320&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), which was 30.7 times bigger than that of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command_Module" class="mw-redirect" title="Apollo Command Module">Apollo Command Module</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-livingandworking_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livingandworking-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo.E2.80.93Soyuz_Test_Project_.281972.E2.80.9375.29">Apollo–Soyuz Test Project (1972–75)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollo%E2%80%93Soyuz_Test_Project" title="Apollo–Soyuz Test Project">Apollo–Soyuz Test Project</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg/220px-Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg/330px-Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg/440px-Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1498" data-file-height="991" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soviet and American crews with spacecraft model, 1975.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 24, 1972, US President <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Nixon" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard M. Nixon">Richard M. Nixon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> Premier <a href="/wiki/Alexei_Kosygin" title="Alexei Kosygin">Alexei Kosygin</a> signed an agreement calling for a joint manned space mission, and declaring intent for all future international manned spacecraft to be capable of docking with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> This authorized the Apollo-Soyuz Test Project (ASTP), involving the rendezvous and docking in Earth orbit of a surplus <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">Apollo Command/Service Module</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> spacecraft. The mission took place in July 1975. This was the last US manned space flight until the first orbital flight of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> in April 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-ASTP_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASTP-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The mission included both joint and separate scientific experiments, and provided useful engineering experience for future joint US–Russian space flights, such as the Shuttle–Mir Program<sup id="cite_ref-Shuttle-MIR_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuttle-MIR-54">[53]</a></sup> and the International Space Station.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Shuttle_program_.281972.E2.80.932011.29">Space Shuttle program (1972–2011)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shuttle-a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shuttle-a.jpg/170px-Shuttle-a.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shuttle-a.jpg/255px-Shuttle-a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shuttle-a.jpg/340px-Shuttle-a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="602" data-file-height="806" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shuttle-a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Launch of a Space Shuttle.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_(7544385084).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg/220px-Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg/330px-Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg/440px-Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1985" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_(7544385084).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mae_Jemison" title="Mae Jemison">Mae Jemison</a> working in Spacelab in 1992. Spacelab was a major NASA collaboration with Europe's space agencies</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> became the major focus of NASA in the late 1970s and the 1980s. Planned as a frequently launchable and mostly reusable vehicle, four space shuttle orbiters were built by 1985. The first to launch, <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a>, did so on April 12, 1981,<sup id="cite_ref-Odyssey_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odyssey-55">[54]</a></sup> the 20th anniversary of the first known human space flight.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Its major components were a <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a> orbiter with an external fuel tank and two solid-fuel launch rockets at its side. The external tank, which was bigger than the spacecraft itself, was the only major component that was not reused. The shuttle could orbit in altitudes of 185–643&#160;km (115–400 <a href="/wiki/Statute_mile" class="mw-redirect" title="Statute mile">miles</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-ShuttleBas_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShuttleBas-57">[56]</a></sup> and carry a maximum payload (to low orbit) of 24,400&#160;kg (54,000&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-AstroShutt_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AstroShutt-58">[57]</a></sup> Missions could last from 5 to 17 days and crews could be from 2 to 8 astronauts.<sup id="cite_ref-ShuttleBas_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShuttleBas-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 20 missions (1983–98) the Space Shuttle carried <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a>, designed in cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA). Spacelab was not designed for independent orbital flight, but remained in the Shuttle's cargo bay as the astronauts entered and left it through an <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AstroSpacelab_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AstroSpacelab-59">[58]</a></sup> Another famous series of missions were the <a href="/wiki/STS-31" title="STS-31">launch</a> and later <a href="/wiki/STS-61" title="STS-61">successful repair</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in 1990 and 1993, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-AstroHST_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AstroHST-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, Russian-American interaction resumed with the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle%E2%80%93Mir_Program" title="Shuttle–Mir Program">Shuttle–Mir</a> missions (1995–1998). Once more an American vehicle docked with a Russian craft, this time a full-fledged space station. This cooperation has continued with Russia and the United States as two of the biggest partners in the largest space station built: the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS). The strength of their cooperation on this project was even more evident when NASA began relying on Russian launch vehicles to service the ISS during the two-year grounding of the shuttle fleet following the 2003 <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">Space Shuttle <i>Columbia</i> disaster</a>.</p>
<p>The Shuttle fleet lost two orbiters and 14 astronauts in two disasters: <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Challenger</a></i> in 1986, and <i>Columbia</i> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-delays1_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delays1-61">[60]</a></sup> While the 1986 loss was mitigated by building the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">Space Shuttle <i>Endeavour</i></a> from replacement parts, NASA did not build another orbiter to replace the <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">second loss</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-delays1_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delays1-61">[60]</a></sup> NASA's Space Shuttle program had 135 missions when the program ended with the successful landing of the Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> at the Kennedy Space Center on July 21, 2011. The program spanned 30 years with over 300 astronauts sent into space.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Space_Station_.281993.E2.80.93present.29">International Space Station (1993–present)</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg/220px-STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg/330px-STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg/440px-STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The International Space Station as seen by the final STS mission</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) combines NASA's <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Freedom" title="Space Station Freedom">Space Station Freedom</a></i> project with the Soviet/Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Mir-2" title="Mir-2">Mir-2</a></i> station, the European <i><a href="/wiki/Columbus_(ISS_module)" title="Columbus (ISS module)">Columbus</a></i> station, and the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Experiment Module">Kibō</a> laboratory module.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSBook_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-63">[62]</a></sup> NASA originally planned in the 1980s to develop <i>Freedom</i> alone, but US budget constraints led to the merger of these projects into a single multi-national program in 1993, managed by NASA, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Federal Space Agency">Russian Federal Space Agency</a> (RKA), the <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency</a> (JAXA), the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA), and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency" title="Canadian Space Agency">Canadian Space Agency</a> (CSA).<sup id="cite_ref-PartStates_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PartStates-64">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-65">[64]</a></sup> The station consists of pressurized modules, external <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">trusses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_arrays" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar arrays">solar arrays</a> and other components, which have been launched by Russian <a href="/wiki/Proton_(rocket)" class="mw-redirect" title="Proton (rocket)">Proton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(rocket_family)" title="Soyuz (rocket family)">Soyuz</a> rockets, and the US Space Shuttles.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSBook_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-63">[62]</a></sup> It is <a href="/wiki/Assembly_of_the_International_Space_Station" title="Assembly of the International Space Station">currently being assembled</a> in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_Orbit" class="mw-redirect" title="Low Earth Orbit">Low Earth Orbit</a>. The on-orbit assembly began in 1998, the completion of the <a href="/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment" title="US Orbital Segment">US Orbital Segment</a> occurred in 2011 and the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment" title="Russian Orbital Segment">Russian Orbital Segment</a> is expected by 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Precise_language" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The launch date of the modules have significantly slipped, and there is now some debate on whether they will ever launch.">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> The ownership and use of the space station is established in intergovernmental treaties and agreements<sup id="cite_ref-ESA-IGA_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-IGA-68">[67]</a></sup> which divide the station into two areas and allow <a href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Federation">Russia</a> to retain full ownership of the Russian Orbital Segment (with the exception of Zarya),<sup id="cite_ref-RSA-MOU_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSA-MOU-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> with the US Orbital Segment allocated between the other international partners.<sup id="cite_ref-ESA-IGA_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-IGA-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Long duration missions to the ISS are referred to as <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions" title="List of International Space Station expeditions">ISS Expeditions</a>. Expedition crew members typically spend approximately six months on the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> The initial expedition crew size was three, temporarily decreased to two following the Columbia disaster. Since May 2009, expedition crew size has been six crew members.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Crew size is expected to be increased to seven, the number the ISS was designed for, once the Commercial Crew Program becomes operational.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> The ISS has been continuously occupied for the past <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7008516421889000000♠</span>16&#160;years and 133&#160;days, having exceeded the previous record held by <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a>; and has been visited by astronauts and cosmonauts from <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_visitors" title="List of International Space Station visitors">15 different nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10th_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10th-74">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The station can be seen from the Earth with the naked eye and, as of 2017, is the largest artificial satellite in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> orbit with a mass and volume greater than that of any previous space station.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttlepresskit_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttlepresskit-76">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> spacecraft delivers crew members, stays docked for their half-year-long missions and then returns them home. Several uncrewed cargo spacecraft service the ISS, they are the Russian <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress spacecraft</a> which has done so since 2000, the European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">Automated Transfer Vehicle</a> (ATV) since 2008, the Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">H-II Transfer Vehicle</a> (HTV) since 2009, the American <a href="/wiki/Dragon_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Dragon spacecraft">Dragon spacecraft</a> since 2012, and the American <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus spacecraft</a> since 2013. The Space Shuttle, before its retirement, was also used for cargo transfer and would often switch out expedition crew members, although it did not have the capability to remain docked for the duration of their stay. Until another US manned spacecraft is ready, crew members will travel to and from the International Space Station exclusively aboard the Soyuz.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> The highest number of people occupying the ISS has been thirteen; this occurred three times during the late Shuttle ISS assembly missions.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ISS program is expected to continue until at least 2020, and may be extended beyond 2028.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_Resupply_Services_.282006.E2.80.93present.29">Commercial Resupply Services (2006–present)</span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Resupply_Services" title="Commercial Resupply Services">Commercial Resupply Services</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:344px;max-width:344px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/150px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg" width="150" height="125" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/225px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/300px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3402" data-file-height="2832" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Dragon is seen being berthed to the ISS in May 2012</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:188px;max-width:188px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg/186px-Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg" width="186" height="124" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg/279px-Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg/372px-Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Standard variant of Cygnus is seen berthed to the ISS in September 2013</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The development of the Commercial Resupply Services (CRS) vehicles began in 2006 with the purpose of creating American commercially operated uncrewed cargo vehicles to service the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> The development of these vehicles was under a fixed price milestone-based program, meaning that each company that received a funded award had a list of milestones with a dollar value attached to them that they didn't receive until after they had successful completed the milestone.<sup id="cite_ref-nasaRendezvous2010-2_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasaRendezvous2010-2-81">[80]</a></sup> Private companies were also required to have some "skin in the game" which refers raising an unspecified amount of private investment for their proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 23, 2008, NASA awarded Commercial Resupply Services contracts to <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation" title="Orbital Sciences Corporation">Orbital Sciences Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> SpaceX uses its <a href="/wiki/Falcon_9_rocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Falcon 9 rocket">Falcon 9 rocket</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dragon_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Dragon spacecraft">Dragon spacecraft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Orbital Sciences uses its <a href="/wiki/Antares_(rocket)" title="Antares (rocket)">Antares</a> rocket and <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus spacecraft</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dragon_C2%2B" title="Dragon C2+">first Dragon resupply mission</a> occurred in May 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_1" class="mw-redirect" title="Cygnus 1">first Cygnus resupply mission</a> occurred in September 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> The CRS program now provides for all America's ISS cargo needs; with the exception of a few vehicle-specific payloads that are delivered on the European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a> and the Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">HTV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_Crew_Program_.282010.E2.80.93present.29">Commercial Crew Program (2010–present)</span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a> (CCDev) program was initiated in 2010 with the purpose of creating American commercially operated crewed spacecraft capable of delivering at least four crew members to the ISS, staying docked for 180 days and then returning them back to Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-Berger_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berger-88">[87]</a></sup> It is hoped that these vehicles could also transport non-NASA customers to private space stations such those planned by <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Like COTS, CCDev is also a fixed price milestone-based developmental program that requires some private investment.<sup id="cite_ref-nasaRendezvous2010-2_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasaRendezvous2010-2-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, NASA announced the winners of the first phase of the program, a total of $50&#160;million was divided among five American companies to foster research and development into human spaceflight concepts and technologies in the private sector. In 2011, the winners of the second phase of the program were announced, $270&#160;million was divided among four companies.<sup id="cite_ref-ft20110418_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ft20110418-90">[89]</a></sup> In 2012, the winners of the third phase of the program were announced, NASA provided $1.1 billion divided among three companies to further develop their crew transportation systems.<sup id="cite_ref-August2012_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-August2012-91">[90]</a></sup> In 2014, the winners of the final round were announced.<sup id="cite_ref-CCtCapBlogAnnounce_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCtCapBlogAnnounce-92">[91]</a></sup> SpaceX's <a href="/wiki/Dragon_V2" class="mw-redirect" title="Dragon V2">Dragon V2</a> (planned to be launched on a <a href="/wiki/Falcon_9_v1.1" title="Falcon 9 v1.1">Falcon 9 v1.1</a>) received a contract valued up to $2.6 billion and Boeing's <a href="/wiki/CST-100" class="mw-redirect" title="CST-100">CST-100</a> (to be launched on an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a>) received a contract valued up to $4.2 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-sn20140921_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140921-93">[92]</a></sup> NASA expects these vehicles to begin transporting humans to the ISS in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-sn20140921_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140921-93">[92]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px">
<div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_(16661791299).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg/270px-SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg" width="180" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg/405px-SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg/540px-SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a></div>
